<div id="review_2233005168" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2233005168" />
    <a title="Adam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1879990-adam"><img alt="Adam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1469320702p2/1879990.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2233005168?book_show_action=true">Nov 07, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam" href="/user/show/1879990-adam">Adam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1879990-adam?shelf=ebook">ebook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1879990-adam?shelf=mongolia">mongolia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1879990-adam?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1879990-adam?shelf=ecofiction">ecofiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1879990-adam?shelf=environmental-history">environmental-history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2233005168" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13809821548065110485">I'm not sure exactly how I should score this. On one hand, I read it for the purpose of researching Mongolian life for my novel project, and it proved to be a treasure trove beyond anything I was anticipating. I was hoping for a few details of life and society as a herder, and somewhat afraid that the 20th century setting would render much of it irrelevant. Instead, it turns out that this book is not just about a boy growing up on the steppe. It's basically a first-person environmental history a</span>
  <span id="freeText13809821548065110485" style="display:none">I'm not sure exactly how I should score this. On one hand, I read it for the purpose of researching Mongolian life for my novel project, and it proved to be a treasure trove beyond anything I was anticipating. I was hoping for a few details of life and society as a herder, and somewhat afraid that the 20th century setting would render much of it irrelevant. Instead, it turns out that this book is not just about a boy growing up on the steppe. It's basically a first-person environmental history and ecology memoir. The protagonist is a Chinese student who spends the first two thirds ourselves the book learning about the ecology of the steppe, focusing on wolves but taking frequent detours into other species like mosquitoes and marmots, with plenty of emphasis on the internal ecology of the herd and a bit about soil and plants and water as well. The brilliant thing about it for me was that the way the real fictionalized Mongolian people in the story talk about wild animals is exactly the kind of thing I was worried would be too on the nose, too Western and anachronistic, for my characters to notice or believe. There is a broad pantheist or monotheist deal going on, but everything in the medium or immediate levels of abstraction is basically conservation science. That was reassuring for me in broad strokes, but it also gave me a lot of information on ecological interactions and processes that other sources had left out.<br /><br />So all of that was brilliant for me. As a novel, though (and the copy I had for some reason insists in its subtitle that it is a novel), it barely qualifies. The reason it took me so long to read wasn't because I kept stopping to take notes. It was because the story has no pull to it at all. Names come and go without establishing who they are or what they mean to anyone. There is a second protagonist distinguishable from the first only in name. The first two thirds of the book are just a sequence of steppe vignettes, mostly interesting but not a coherent story by any means.<br /><br />It's only in the last third when things start to find a dramatic conflict. That's when we shift gears from immersion in historical Mongolian lifestyles to conflict with encroaching Chinese settlers. Of course, it's too late at that point to make a real novel out of it, but it does have some built-in tension because the themes that it explores are so inherently poignant to people like me. Arrogant, ignorant settlers displacing both an indigenous way of life and destroying the vibrant ecosystems that surround them. It just hurts.<br /><br />It was quite interesting, though, to see that conflict play out between Mongols and Chinese. The setup and the story has a bit of "Dances with Wolves" about it, in the outsider perspective on and indigenous grassland community doomed by the encroachment of an agricultural settler colonial state. And the wolves, of course. But for a few reasons, the dynamic felt totally different. For one thing, both Mongols and Chinese are committed to communism. One of the biggest points the book makes is not so much that communism is bad per se, but that the bureaucratic structure made it very difficult for people with on the ground experience to influence policy that affected them. The fact that the conflict between philosophies plays out within a fairly claustrophobic ideology kind of stifles any overt confrontation. Another interesting change is that the two peoples have been living side-by-side for a very long time. Far from exacerbating strife through some kind of ancient ethnic hatred, though, that shared history makes this feel like less of a frontier than in practice it actually is. <br /><br />The main character is initially interested in wolves because the Mongols constantly talk about how much they've learned from them. The way he talks about Mongol and Chinese history feels very coarse and blunt, speaking very matter-of-factly about which races have achieved more and why. He comes to the conclusion that wolves have shaped and taught Mongols and their herds to be powerful and vibrant in a way the Han Chinese are not (he always calls them Han Chinese). It's an essentializing and environmental determinist story that isn't particularly thoughtful, and echoes plenty of Western noble savage tropes. And maybe this is a flaw of the way Rong tells the story, but it just never feels like a clash of civilizations or even cultures. The fact that everyone is ostensibly on the same side, but the Mongols don't really have much say in what happens, no way to use their wisdom and experience to achieve the stated goal of their shared faction, means that the conflict is ultimately the same. It's just somehow more submerged and insidious. Interesting, at least. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13809821548065110485" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

I'm not sure I get the interest beyond the national borders, though.<br/><br/>Because it's not a great story--it's a conventional story--the characters are never more than stereotypes, the dialogue (and prose generally) c</span>
  <span id="freeText7476509519988940072" style="display:none">I'm missing something, I guess.<br /><br />I mean, I get why the book might have been popular in China; I don't really know the issues, but there are long philosophical discussions on the nature of the Chinese character--meaning the character of Han Chinese--which could theoretically strike a chord. I'm not sure I get the interest beyond the national borders, though.<br /><br />Because it's not a great story--it's a conventional story--the characters are never more than stereotypes, the dialogue (and prose generally) can be wooden (which may be the result of the translation, or national differences in prose), and the book is so didactic. It's Never Cry Wolf--a good book--by way of the pedantic Dances with Wolves.<br /><br />The story is about a Chinese student who goes to live on the Mongolian grasslands at the start of the Cultural Revolution, in the 1960s and--as is repeated in front matter, end matter, and the back of the book--roughly autobiographical. Chan is a dedicated student to the wise old Mongolian shepherd, who teaches him about the ecology of area, and, crucially, how important wolves are. (Without wolves, all the grazers would destroy the grasslands and turn it into desert). And so how the shepherds are in delicate balance with the wolves, killing them sometimes, defending their sheep against the wolves, but also respecting them.<br /><br />Wolf Totem won the Man Asian prize in 2007--the first time it was given out--and the award notification excerpted on the back mentions how complex the ecological description is. And Chan repeatedly says the same. Except, there's really nothing complex here. It's a lecture, given over and over and over again.<br /><br />Chan surprises the Mongol's in how much he likes the wolves, and Mongolian modes of life--except that he takes it too far and, with another student, raids a wolf den and steals a cub to raise. Supposedly, they are doing something scientific--and I don't think the book necessarily disagrees with them, but rather suggests that Mongolian wolves should not be experimented upon. It's a weird sort of science, though: the prevailing argument--the book is as much argumentative essay as story, with long, didactic speeches--is that the wolf inspired the civilization of the nomadic Mongolian culture, and made them strong--that's why Genghis Khan conquered so much of the world. And so Chan and his friend determine, based on watching the animals, that wolves must have taught Mongols, for example, the wisdom of trenches in warfare. (I really didn't get that.)<br /><br />At the same time the story is about the integrity of Mongolian shepherding practices, it is also a compare-and contrast discourse on Chinese character versus Mongolian character. The Han are the last of the great agricultural civilizations, we are meant to understand, the other three having gone extinct. Europeans are barbarians, as are Mongolians, and the Chinese need to inject some of that into their character. Instead, they are too staid, too static, too populous, devouring the world's resources without regard for others or the future.<br /><br />This last point is shown in the depiction of the heartless Han who move into the area, start destroying the wolves, and also all the other animals--in hopes of using the grassland to raise meat and horses, but only turning the region into a desert, except for a few small oases where traditional practices are maintained.<br /><br />As should be obvious from the set up of the book, Chan eventually has to confront his poor decision to keep the wolf, which provides the book its emotional climax.<br /><br />The novel isn't bad per se. It's perfectly cromulent. There are way too many lectures, and plenty of other foibles--the author clearly really wanted to write an essay, but, for whatever reason, chose to do a novel , instead. It's engaging enough, and parts of it work as well as any narrative. But there are no surprises, everybody performs exactly as expected. I found myself skimming large sections and not missing anything.<br /><br />I don't feel bad for having read it, but not better for it, either, and suspect in a year or two won't remember it at all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7476509519988940072" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1911151003" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1911151003" />
    <a title="Moushine Zahr" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53509133-moushine-zahr"><img alt="Moushine Zahr" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1457457507p2/53509133.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1911151003?book_show_action=true">May 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Moushine Zahr" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Moushine Zahr" href="/user/show/53509133-moushine-zahr">Moushine Zahr</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9782849410813" href="/book/show/2922030-le-totem-du-loup">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1911151003" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3725293210505496653">From the first page of the first chapter of this novel, the reader is directly immersed into the story and the action in the middle of the Inner Mongolia's steppe.<br/>From the first page, I was captivated by the story, fascinated by the location of the story and intrigued by what's next.<br/>From the first page, I felt this novel was going to be amazing to read and it surpassed my expectations.<br/><br/>This novel was written by a Chinese Han and the story is set in the Steppe of Inner Mongolia during the Cultural</span>
  <span id="freeText3725293210505496653" style="display:none">From the first page of the first chapter of this novel, the reader is directly immersed into the story and the action in the middle of the Inner Mongolia's steppe.<br />From the first page, I was captivated by the story, fascinated by the location of the story and intrigued by what's next.<br />From the first page, I felt this novel was going to be amazing to read and it surpassed my expectations.<br /><br />This novel was written by a Chinese Han and the story is set in the Steppe of Inner Mongolia during the Cultural Revolution in the late 60's and early 70's. This uniquely amazing novel can be read in 3 different ways simultanously:<br /><br />- the novel is a semi-autobiography of the author, who deliberately chose to be sent in Inner-Mongolia to be "re-educated" by the poor shepherds/hunters living in the natural and difficult steppe environment. The author wrote about his experience living there during 10 years, learning from the old Mongolian shephers about the Steppe, its human and animal residents, and its climate, falling in love with the ways of the Mongolian Shephers and their respect for the Wolves, and his suffering due to contradictory feelings and his uselessness in saving the Steppe.<br /><br />- the novel is about the Inner-Mongolian Steppe, all its residents, its dangers, its natural beauty, its thousand years of existence in great equilibrium. The author reminded us how it was from these same Steppe that several dynasties invaded mainland China easily despite the Great Wall to conquer it during centuries and of course he reminded us that Gengis Khan who conquered half of the World orginated from Inner-Mongolia and his military strategies were inspired by the wolves of the Steppe. Unfortunately, despite ten thousand years of existance and its conquering history, the Steppe and its animal residents were exterminated within a few years by the invasion of people unfamiliar with the ways of the Steppe, Han and Inner-Mongolian farmers alike, who were ignorant, arrogant, selfish, nearsighed, applying policies made thousands of miles away in the name of economic progress. However, the economic progress was short lived and the Steppe became a desert at the doorstep of Beijing.<br /><br />- The novel also put questions, discussions and thoughts to the readers about humans, animals, and humanity. Is there still inside each human part of his long forgotten life as a nomadic shepherd? How much do we take inspiration from animals? What kind of civilization and/or empire can conquer the world while others disappear? What category of humans succeed in surviving while others fail and die? The author wrote also about how he saw Chinese Han History through a totally new and different angle after learning and adopting the Inner-Mongolian ways of life and hunting. <br /><br />WARNING: If you're an animal lover and have a very sensitive heart, you may not want to read this novel because it contains many gruesome violent and bloody scenes against animals by wolves and by humans. <br /><br />This novel is:<br /><br />- one of the best Chinese novels I've ever read,<br />- one of the best novels I've read this year 2017,<br />- one of the must-read novel in my list of "must-read" books in a lifetime if I had one.<br /><br />Thanks to this novel, I've learned so much about Inner-Mongolia, the lives of shephers and/or hunters, the disappeared ecosystem of Steppe, and of course about wolves and humans. No wonder that this novel sold 20 millions copies in China after its first publication in 2004. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3725293210505496653" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1326206240069173116">Spoiler alert. Here is a summary of this novel:<br/><br/>Wolves tearing the guts out of gazelles. Dogs ripping the guts out of wolves. Wolves tearing open live horses. Men gunning down wolves. Detailed anthropology and biology of the Inner Mongolian grasslands. Oh, and while they're at it, men massacre swans.<br/><br/>It stopped any pretense of being a story by around page 100, and it turned into a rather repetitive anthropological treatise about human relationships with wolves, which was not nearly as interesting </span>
  <span id="freeText1326206240069173116" style="display:none">Spoiler alert. Here is a summary of this novel:<br /><br />Wolves tearing the guts out of gazelles. Dogs ripping the guts out of wolves. Wolves tearing open live horses. Men gunning down wolves. Detailed anthropology and biology of the Inner Mongolian grasslands. Oh, and while they're at it, men massacre swans.<br /><br />It stopped any pretense of being a story by around page 100, and it turned into a rather repetitive anthropological treatise about human relationships with wolves, which was not nearly as interesting as it might have been. I was inspired to read the book after seeing the movie, which plucked characters and a few events from the book and made it into an actual, fascinating, beautifully acted and filmed story. I quit reading at about 60 percent through. There is a lot of great contemporary Chinese literature in English translation. This is not among them.<br /><br />Skip the book. Definitely see the movie.</span>

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Qbn8Fo5WppA5JqlzYW0x4CTKXlIwECaG2Cti9yNr48111v3Q4lhoGH5C9RJ9g9h2OBzmaFthCT4+vvX2CJvcLQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('LqFbysLC8J1UFcvfKgcb2ARr7TIeMeG0Ym17A6C1U0EazloMrsw+FRNxl7426fJOGL1VCHVAzgyE+OwCi0VsoQ==')})
  }
//]]>

As comically mismatched teenagers, Baldy Li, a sex-obsessed ne’er-do-well, and Song Gang, his bookish, sensitive stepbrother, vow that they will always be brothers--a bond they will struggle to maintain over the years as they weather the ups and downs of rivalry in love and making and losing millions in the new China. Shattered to discover that his favorite son was actually born of a liaison between his wife and a neighbor, he suffers his greatest indignity, while his wife is publicly scorned as a prostitute. Although the poverty and betrayals of Mao\'s regime have drained him, Xu Sanguan ultimately finds strength in the blood ties of his family.
